10.1016/j.jmp.2016.12.002
Random utility models (RUM) focus on the probability that a given alternative is the best choice among a finite set of available alternatives; see, e.g.,  Anderson, de Palma, and Thisse (1992) ,  Ben-Akiva and Lerman (1985) ,  Manski (1977)  and  McFadden (1974, 2001) . A wide literature has emerged and has been used for applications in several disciplines (decision theory, demography, labor economics, marketing, regional science and urban economics, transportation science, etc.).

While such literature has been (and still is) mainly focused on the best choice, several authors starting with  Luce (1959) , have explored more comprehensive approaches which do not exclusively focus on the best alternative. A new branch of the literature, initiated by  Louviere and Woodworth (1983)  and by  Finn and Louviere (1992) , has developed experimental design methods to extract information about best and worst choices and shown that such information allows to better elicit individual preferences. An early axiomatic approach of best, worst, and best–worst choice probabilities was proposed by  Marley (1968) .

More recently,  Marley (2010)  and  Marley and Louviere (2005)  have developed a general axiomatic approach to represent the worst choice probability as well as the best–worst choice probability, that is, the probability that an alternative  
                         x 
                       is the best choice and an alternative  
                         y 
                       is the worst one in a given choice set. A recent survey on these advances is available in the elegant monograph of  Louviere, Flynn, and Marley (2015) . We prove various new relations between best, worst, and best–worst choices, and apply them to specific RUM cases.

More specifically, the main contributions of the paper are as listed below:  
                         
                            • 
                            For any RUM (under very mild assumptions), we derive a formula linking best to worst choice probabilities. The formula in the main theorem ( Theorem 5 ) relies on a standard combinatorial property, the inclusion–exclusion principle of  Poincaré (1912, pp. 58–66) , 
                                  1 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                                  The inclusion–exclusion principle dates back to 1718 and is attributed to de Moivre. 
                                and states that a worst choice probability can be expressed as an alternating sum of best choice probabilities. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Theorem 5  is applied to derive the worst choice probabilities of RUM where the utilities are independent extreme value or even generalized extreme value (GEV) random variables, with best choice probabilities satisfying the multinomial logit (MNL) model and the GEV model, respectively. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            We use results of  Marley and Louviere (2005)  to obtain closed form expressions for the best–worst choice probabilities in the case of a RUM yielding the MNL model. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            With slightly stronger assumptions, we derive a general formula for the best–worst probabilities (see  Theorem 10 ), which contains double integrals. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            This general formula is applied to obtain expressions for the best–worst choice probabilities, for the RUM yielding the GEV model for the best choice probabilities, which contain single integrals. No closed forms exist, to the best of our knowledge, except for the special case of the independent extreme value distributed utilities. 
                         
                       The paper is organized as follows: Section   2  introduces the main notations. In Section   3 ,  Lemma 1  shows that the dummy variable indicating whether or not an alternative  
                         x 
                       is the worst alternative in a given set (worst choice indicator) can be written as an alternating sum of dummies (best choice indicators) for whether or not  
                         x 
                       is the best alternative over appropriately chosen subsets, and conversely.  Lemma 2  establishes simple relations between the dummy for whether or not  
                         x 
                       and  
                         y 
                       are jointly the best–worst choices (best–worst choice indicator) and the best and the worst choice indicators.  Lemma 1  is then used to derive identities for maximum and minimum utilities ( Proposition 3 ) and their expectation ( Proposition 4 ). In Section   4 , we derive the main theorem ( Theorem 5 ) and use the first identities of this paper to provide an interpretation of the Block–Marschak polynomials ( Proposition 6 ). In Section   5 , the main theorem is used to derive a new worst choice probability formula corresponding to a counterpart of the MNL formula ( Corollary 7 ); then, applying a result of  Marley and Louviere (2005) , a closed form expression for the best–worst choice probabilities is also derived ( Proposition 8 ) for the same model. The worst choice probabilities for the model with best choice probabilities satisfying the GEV model are derived in  Corollary 9 . Section   6  introduces, under slightly stronger assumptions than the general RUM framework, explicit expressions (up to double integrals) of the best–worst choice probabilities ( Theorem 10 ) which are then applied to the GEV case ( Proposition 11 ). Section   7  concludes.

For any RUM (under very mild assumptions), we derive a formula linking best to worst choice probabilities. The formula in the main theorem ( Theorem 5 ) relies on a standard combinatorial property, the inclusion–exclusion principle of  Poincaré (1912, pp. 58–66) , 
                                  1 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                                  The inclusion–exclusion principle dates back to 1718 and is attributed to de Moivre. 
                                and states that a worst choice probability can be expressed as an alternating sum of best choice probabilities.

Theorem 5  is applied to derive the worst choice probabilities of RUM where the utilities are independent extreme value or even generalized extreme value (GEV) random variables, with best choice probabilities satisfying the multinomial logit (MNL) model and the GEV model, respectively.

We use results of  Marley and Louviere (2005)  to obtain closed form expressions for the best–worst choice probabilities in the case of a RUM yielding the MNL model.

With slightly stronger assumptions, we derive a general formula for the best–worst probabilities (see  Theorem 10 ), which contains double integrals.

This general formula is applied to obtain expressions for the best–worst choice probabilities, for the RUM yielding the GEV model for the best choice probabilities, which contain single integrals. No closed forms exist, to the best of our knowledge, except for the special case of the independent extreme value distributed utilities.

In this section, some useful identities relating the best, worst, and best–worst choice indicators, which are random variables defined on  
                         Ω 
                       with probability measure  
                         Pr 
                      , are stated. Some of the identities are intended almost everywhere (a.e.) with respect to  
                         Pr 
                      . We also provide identities relating the (expected) achieved utility corresponding to the best choice vs. the worst choice.

We first derive an identity which expresses the worst choice indicators as a function of best choice indicators, and conversely, the best choice indicators as a function of worst choice indicators. We have:

We now provide simple identities relating the best–worst choice indicators and the best and worst choice indicators, which will turn to be useful in the sequel.

When alternative utilities have finite expectation, the expected maximum utility can be used as a welfare measure for some RUM (see   Small & Rosen, 1981 ). An alternative measure, the expected minimum utility, corresponds to the expected benefit of the worst situation. We derive identities for expected maximum vs. minimum utilities which are similar to the identities proved in  Lemma 1 .

We are now ready to derive the main theorem of the paper which provides relations between the best and worst choice probabilities. Moreover, identities proved in this paper can be used to provide an easy interpretation of the Block–Marschak polynomials.

                          respectively. The functional form given by Eq.  (16)  involves a sum of best choice probabilities (within subsets of alternatives) that alternate in sign. The sign is positive when the subset has odd cardinality and negative when the subset has even cardinality.

Theorem 5  states that the worst choice probability in a given choice set can be obtained from best choice probabilities calculated within different appropriately chosen subsets of alternatives, and vice versa.

Results proved in this paper can be used to provide an interpretation of the Block–Marschak polynomials (see   Block & Marschak, 1960 ). These polynomials are defined, for

The exact explicit Formula  (28)  for the best–worst choice probabilities are new in the RUM literature. However, even in the GEV framework (with the exception of independent extreme value random utilities), no such closed forms exist for the best–worst choice probabilities, as we will see in Section   6 .

McFadden (1978)  has proposed an extension of the MNL model known as GEV model which is obtained from the same assumptions than the RUM introduced in Section   5.1 , but now, correlation among alternative utilities is allowed.

Eq.  (31)  is obtained by substituting the GEV model choice probabilities given by Eq.  (30)  into Identity  (16) . □

In a standard RUM framework, a new theorem provides an identity for the best–worst choice probabilities involving in general a two-dimensional integral, generalizing the formula obtained for a consistent extreme value RUM in  Proposition 8 . We then apply this theorem to the consistent generalized extreme value RUM case.

Previous identities and integration techniques are now used to derive a general identity for the best–worst choice probabilities.

Explicit solutions for the best–worst choice probabilities exist in the case of independent Gumbel utilities yielding the MNL model (see  Proposition 8 ). Even if some computations suggest that the answer is negative, we cannot say for sure whether the single integrals given by Eq.  (34)  can be rewritten in closed form for generalizations of that model with best choice probabilities satisfying a NMNL (nested multinomial logit) model or a GenL (choice set generation logit) model proposed by  Swait (2001) .

This paper provides an identity for the worst choice probabilities which can be expressed as a function of the best choice probabilities for RUM under very mild assumptions. The key ingredient to get the connection between best and worst choice probabilities is the inclusion–exclusion principle.

Louviere et al. (2015)  have shown that the worst choice probabilities or a combination of best and worst choice probabilities may be more efficient econometrically to elicit preferences than best choice probabilities. Simple numerical simulations suggest that, with MNL and probit models, better estimates can be obtained when using the worst rather than the best choices (see   de Palma, Kilani, & Laffond, 2013 ). Our results allow to write down explicitly the likelihood functions for estimators obtained from worst and best–worst data. Their exact statistical properties are difficult to obtain and are left for future research.

We thank S. Anderson, P. Chiappori, C. Holt, J. Lainé, J. Louviere, and in particular A. Marley who provided us useful suggestions and continuous encouragements.
